<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="a88fc894da3244c29d3e86abad311863"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="a88fc894da3244c29d3e86abad311863">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f65944ef-53aa-46fb-a6b9-a793273072f8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d280575604" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>TFEP reconstructs the Helmholtz plane on HC anode and boosts a robust F-rich SEI film by strongly adsorbing on the surface of HC and dragging the outer PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, thus greatly enhances the mechanical stability and the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics of the electrode/electrolyte interface.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/10715450-5075-4692-b790-55a98c7c43dc/anie202407717-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/10715450-5075-4692-b790-55a98c7c43dc/anie202407717-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b3d4c9c9-b113-4ddc-b5af-d69d040bddf1/anie202407717-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/10715450-5075-4692-b790-55a98c7c43dc/anie202407717-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d280576187" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon (HC) is the most commonly used anode material in sodium-ion batteries. However, the solid-electrolyte-interface (SEI) layer formed in carbonate ester-based electrolytes has an imperceptible dissolution tendency and a sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, resulting in an unsatisfactory performance of HC anode. Given that electrode/electrolyte interface property is highly dependent on the configuration of Helmholtz plane, we filtrated proper solvents by PFBE (PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion binding energy) and CAE (carbon absorption energy) and disclosed the function of chosen TFEP to reconstruct the Helmholtz plane and regulate the SEI film on HC anode. Benefiting from the preferential adsorption tendency on HC surface and strong anion-dragging interaction of TFEP, a robust and thin anion-derived F-rich SEI film is established, which greatly enhances the mechanical stability and the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics of the electrode/electrolyte interface. The rationally designed TFEP-based electrolyte endows Na||HC half-cell and 2.8 Ah HC||Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> pouch cell with excellent rate capability, long cycle life, high safety and low-temperature adaptability. It is believed that this insightful recognition of tuning interface properties will pave a new avenue in the design of compatible electrolyte for low-cost, long-life, and high-safe sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202407717-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202407717-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion battery (LIB) currently stands as state-of-art battery technology,<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0001_R_d280575594e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#anie202407717-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0003_R_d280575594e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nature">3</a></sup></span> and occupies a dominant position in the energy storage market from watt-hours (Wh) to megawatt-hours (MWh) grade. Whereas concerns have been raised of late regarding the limited reserves and the escalating price of lithium resources. These issues pose a significant challenge in meeting the increasing demand for grid-scale energy storage systems.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0004_R_d280575594e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Energy Lett.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202407717-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0005_R_d280575594e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Rev.">5</a></sup></span> In this respect, sodium-ion battery (SIB), sharing a similar energy storage mechanism with LIBs, offers a promising alternative, stemming from the abundance of sodium resources and their cost-effectiveness.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0006_R_d280575594e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Rev.">6</a></sup></span> Additionally, SIBs are deemed to exhibit superior ionic diffusion capacity and enhanced safety features compared to LIBs, positioning them favorably for industrialization in energy storage fields.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0007_R_d280575594e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202407717-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0008_R_d280575594e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To attain the requisites for practical commercial application, ongoing efforts focus on advancing electrode materials and electrolytes for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0009_R_d280575594e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Storage Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#anie202407717-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0011_R_d280575594e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nano Energy">11</a></sup></span> At present, hard carbon (HC) emerges as the most ideal anode material, driven by its high specific capacity (over 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), low working potential, and cost-effective production process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0012_R_d280575594e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a>-<a href="#anie202407717-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0014_R_d280575594e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Environ. Sci.">14</a></sup></span> Although ether-based electrolytes or several ionic liquid-based electrolytes exhibit better HC/electrolyte interface properties, the limited electrochemical window and operation stability under room-temperature conditions seriously limit their practical application.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0015_R_d280575594e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span> Hence, carbonate-based electrolytes still retain their top preference in SIBs as in LIBs owing to their cost-effectiveness and accessibility. However, extensive studies have consistently demonstrated a mismatch between the HC anode and carbonate-based electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0016_R_d280575594e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">16</a>, <a href="#anie202407717-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0017_R_d280575594e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Environ. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> Specifically, as for low-concentration carbonate-based electrolytes (LCEs), the composition of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) primarily comprises organic species, such as sodium dicarbonates ((ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na)<sub>2</sub>), semicarbonates (ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na).<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0018_R_d280575594e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Energy">18</a>, <a href="#anie202407717-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0019_R_d280575594e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">19</a></sup></span> These components can offer flexibility and favorable conductivity, whereas their poor mechanical property renders them susceptible to crushing during cycling. Moreover, the significant solubility of organic species results in continuous decomposition of electrolytes and the formation of new SEI film during cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0020_R_d280575594e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy">20</a>, <a href="#anie202407717-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0021_R_d280575594e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a></sup></span> These side reactions would lead to substantial gas evolution and a subpar cycling performance. Besides, the common carbonate ester-based electrolytes are highly flammable,<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0022_R_d280575594e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#anie202407717-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0024_R_d280575594e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Energy Lett.">24</a></sup></span> which bring nonnegligible safety hazard to battery system. Hence, designing flammable-retardant electrolyte systems for SIBs which enable to enhance the intrinsic safety, improve the electrode/electrolyte interface properties and minimize SEI dissolution during cycling has become a top priority.</p>
         
         <p>As well recognized, the robust and uniform SEI is the key to maintaining the compatibility of the electrode/electrolyte interface, while the properties of SEI depend on its composition. Inorganic species like NaF and NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> (arising from the decomposition of anions) have been identified with low solubility in organic electrolytes, thereby enhancing the integrity and stability of the SEI layer.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0025_R_d280575594e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a>, <a href="#anie202407717-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0026_R_d280575594e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Environ. Sci.">26</a></sup></span> Given the poor solubility of nitrates in esters, constructing an electrode/electrolyte interface rich in fluorine seems to be a reliable approach to stabilize the long cycle life of SIBs. Approaches such as developing high-concentration electrolytes (HCEs) or high molar ratio electrolytes (HMREs) have been demonstrated the effectiveness in forming an anion-derived F-rich SEI.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0027_R_d280575594e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Mater.">27</a>-<a href="#anie202407717-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0029_R_d280575594e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Phys. Chem. C">29</a></sup></span> However, the high viscosity and low ionic conductivity of HCEs and HMREs trigger sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics and significantly worsened rate/cycle performance.</p>
         
         <p>To reconcile the swift ion transport kinetics observed in LCEs with the excellent interfacial properties in HCEs, an in-depth understanding and rational design of the interface between HC anode and LCEs become imperative. The electron-rich surface of the HC anode during discharge tends to push the anions to the outer Helmholtz layer due to the Coulomb repulsion, instead the linear solvent with weaker polarity remains in the inner Helmholtz plane (IHP) and decomposes as the root component, resulting in solvent-derived SEI film and poor cycle life.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0030_R_d280575594e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">30</a></sup></span> Therefore, rearranging the solvent/anion on the Helmholtz plane by electrolyte engineering aiming at constructing a stable SEI film is a feasible strategy to address the incompatibility issue of LCEs with HC anode.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we proposed a novel strategy to reconstruct the IHP of the HC anode (as shown in Scheme <a href="#anie202407717-fig-5001">1</a>A). Firstly, introducing solvent that could preferentially absorb on HC surface into the electrolyte system, subsequently, leveraging its strong interaction with anions to draw anions into IHP, optimizing the Helmholtz layer and consequently forming an anion-induced fluorinated (F)-rich interface (Scheme <a href="#anie202407717-fig-5001">1</a>B). Moreover, the solvent is also expected to own flame-retardant characteristics so as to enhance the battery safety. As a proof of concept, tris(2,2,2-trifluoroethyl) phosphate (TFEP) was selected from eleven typical flame-retardant solvents. As expected, the introduction of TFEP effectively reconstructs the IHP and promotes the formation of a robust and thin F-rich SEI film on the surface of the HC anode, which manifests greatly enhanced mechanical stability, reduced SEI solubility, and accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics, as evidenced by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS), atomic force microscope (AFM) and electrochemical impendence spectrum (EIS). Notably, the rationally designed TFEP-based electrolyte endows the Na||HC half-cell and 2.8 Ah HC||Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> pouch cell with long cycle life, excellent rate capability, and favorable low-temperature adaptability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202407717-fig-5001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e1f94896-0a2b-4663-8709-184d03a2b01d/anie202407717-fig-5001-m.jpg"><picture>

         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202407717-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202407717-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202407717-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202407717-sec-0003-title">2.1 Solvent Selection</h3>
            
            <p>To achieve the aforementioned goal, solvents used to regulate electrolytes should possess the following properties: (i) high hard carbon absorption energy (CAE), hence solvents could preferentially absorb on the surface of HC and dominate the property of Helmholtz plane; (ii) strong anion (PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>) binding energy (PFBE), the absorbed solvents could drag PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions into IHP by the strong dipole force (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0001">1A</a>). Hence, we conducted an exhaustive assessment of eleven flame-retardant solvents (including phosphate esters, fluorinated carbonate esters, fluorinated ethers, phosphonitriles, and amides). The LUMO energy level along with chemical structure of solvents, CAE and PFBE of solvents were assessed by density functional theory (DFT) and molecular dynamics (MD) simulations. To diminish the influence of human factors, fifty configurations were randomly selected for each solvent to identify the configuration with the lowest energy, the detailed outcomes are presented in Figure S1B–E.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202407717-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/34402fcf-ef7f-48d7-b1c0-07531b6502df/anie202407717-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/34402fcf-ef7f-48d7-b1c0-07531b6502df/anie202407717-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e6135829-d6a9-478b-b5a5-ed02521700f4/anie202407717-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/34402fcf-ef7f-48d7-b1c0-07531b6502df/anie202407717-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202407717-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202407717" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(A) Key factors of solvent selection; (B) LUMO energy levels of eleven typical flame-retardant solvents; (C–D) PFBE and CAE of different solvents; (E) Two-dimensional Figure for solvent selection, while X axis is PFBE and Y axis is CAE.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0001">1B</a>, among all the solvents, fluorinated solvents stood out due to their inherently lower LUMO energy levels, suggesting a propensity for decomposition during cycling, which could throw a considerable influence on the interfacial properties. In terms of PFBE, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0001">1C</a> and Figure S1, TFEP emerged as a frontrunner with an exceptionally low value of −0.96 eV. Even compared to other typical solvent (like PC, DEC, Figure S4A and Figure S5), TFEP also exhibits remarkable superiority. This superiority is primarily attributed to its high fluorine content and symmetric molecular structure, which stabilize the interactions with PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions. Turning to the metrics of CAE (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0001">1D</a> and Figure S2), TFEP, despite not leading, exhibits a commendable CAE (−0.59 eV), comparable with TEP (−0.62 eV). This marginal CAE decrement observed in TFEP, relative to TEP, may be ascribed to the steric hindrance imparted by fluorination. Similarly, TFEP obviously outperforms other common solvents (Figure S4B and Figure S6). Therefore, based on two-dimensional combinatorial screening of CAE and PFBE, TFEP was determined as the optimized solvent for further investigation (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0001">1E</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202407717-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202407717-sec-0004-title">2.2 Reconstruction of IHP on HC Surface</h3>
            
            <p>Different from the graphite anode in LIBs, HC anode exhibits a favorable electrochemical compatibility with propylene carbonate (PC), hence PC-based electrolytes with wider liquid range have been commonly used for SIBs in previous works. In this context, NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC/DEC (Na<sup>+</sup>/solvent MR=1 : 10, PC/DEC MR=1 : 1, PD) was chosen as the base electrolyte, and the optimal electrolyte formula was expressed as NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC/TFEP/DEC (Na<sup>+</sup>/solvent MR=1 : 10, PC/TFEP/DEC MR=1 : 1 : 1, PTD).</p>
            
            <p>The bulk solvation structures of Na<sup>+</sup> in PD and PTD electrolyte are explored by the combination of nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy and MD simulation. The coordinated condition of solvents and anions are presented in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0002">2A</a>–<a href="#anie202407717-fig-0002">B</a> and the typical solvation models of Na<sup>+</sup> in different electrolytes are shown in Figure S7. Clearly, there is only a slight increase in the number of coordinated anions with Na<sup>+</sup> (both contact ion pair, CIP) after the addition of TFEP, indicating that TFEP has negligible impact on the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> for bulk electrolytes. The deduction is further proved by NMR. Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0002">2C</a> shows <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra of NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC, PD, and PTD electrolytes. The peak located at 0 ppm corresponds to the signal of Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> in DMSO (reference sample). Remarkably, the <sup>23</sup>Na spectra shift to a lower frequency after the addition of DEC, indicative of an enhanced electron-cloud density around Na<sup>+</sup>. Whereas the further introduction of TFEP leads to a slight shift towards a higher field, suggesting a minor change in the solvation chemistry of Na<sup>+</sup>.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202407717-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/571dd634-7893-413a-b971-d64a56246a75/anie202407717-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/571dd634-7893-413a-b971-d64a56246a75/anie202407717-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c442e551-1aed-41bd-aeff-52a1c1b0597b/anie202407717-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/571dd634-7893-413a-b971-d64a56246a75/anie202407717-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202407717-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202407717" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>MD simulations of solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup>. RDFs and coordination numbers of (A) PD; (B) PTD electrolyte. Solid lines: radial distribution functions (RDFs); dot lines: coordinated numbers; (C) <sup>23</sup>Na spectrum in NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC, NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC/DEC and NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC/TFEP/DEC electrolytes; (D–E) Number densities of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions under PZC and negative polarization (−1.5 V vs. PZC) in PD and PTD electrolytes; (F–G) The calculation of EDLC and differential capacitance of PD and PTD electrolytes; (H) The simplified models regarding to the working mechanism of TFEP.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Given that the electrode-electrolyte interface is pivotal in electrochemical reactions, the electrode steric effect and electrode polarization significantly alter the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0031_R_d280575594e813" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Sci.">31</a></sup></span> Therefore, single changes in the solvation structure in the bulk electrolyte inadequately elucidate the substantial performance gaps between the PTD and PD electrolytes. MD simulations with constant potential method were further utilized to simulate the distribution status alongside the electrode/electrolyte interfaces of solvents and anions. Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0002">2D</a> and Figure S8A demonstrates that under the potential of zero charge (PZC), the number densities of PC and DEC within the inner Helmholtz layer in PTD notably diminish compared to those in the PD electrolyte. Conversely, there is a rise in the number densities of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions and TFEP. Furthermore, under negative polarization (−1.5 V vs. PZC), the number densities of TFEP change little (Figure S8B). However, there is a significant difference in PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions. That is, in the PD electrolyte, almost no anions exist in the inner Helmholtz layer owing to the strong electrostatic repulsion. In contrast, a considerable portion of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions still resides in the inner Helmholtz layer in the PTD electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0002">2E</a>). Considering that TFEP is the only difference between the two electrolytes, we hypothesize the root cause for the huge difference is the absorption of TFEP on the surface of HC anode. The strong dipole interaction force existing in TFEP and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions could strongly pull PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, forcing them to tarry in the inner Helmholtz layer. The participation of TFEP and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions on IHP is then studied experimentally.</p>
            
            <p>The atomic schematic proposed by MD simulation was also verified by cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurement on a HC||HC symmetrical cell. The electrochemical double layer capacitance (EDLC) of HC electrode in PD and PTD electrolytes were tested within the non-Faradaic region (Figure S9). Linear fitting results reveal that the EDLC is 159.9 μF cm<sup>−2</sup> in PD electrolyte, by contrast significantly decreases to 61.4 μF cm<sup>−2</sup> in PTD (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0002">2F</a>), reflecting the increased distance of the Stern layer due to the adsorption of TFEP on the HC electrode and its interaction with PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions in the inner Helmholtz layer.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0032_R_d280575594e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Energy Storage Mater.">32</a></sup></span> Subsequent differential capacitance testing was employed to examine the variations in charge distribution at the interface in different electrolytes (the model of three-electrode cell is shown in Figure S10). It is observed that the lowest point of the capacitance curve, often regarded as the PZC, is situated at 2.35 V in PD electrolyte. However, with the addition of TFEP, this point shifts to 2.22 V, indicating that TFEP alters the structure of the EDL.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0033_R_d280575594e860" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>Considering the significant impact of the pore nature of HC on the Helmholtz plane, we synthesized two other types of HC with different pore structures (named as HC-2 and HC-3, the detailed synthesis methods can be seen in the Experimental Methods, the HC previously used in this work is named as HC-1) for further investigation. The specific surface area and pore structure of three different HC materials were investigated using N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption experiments. As shown in Figure S11, the specific surface areas calculated based on Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method using N<sub>2</sub> adsorption isotherms are 15.0, 21.4, and 179.2 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for HC-1, HC-2 and HC-3. The pore size distributions of different HC are depicted in Figure S12B–D. The three samples with distinct differences in pore size distribution and pore volume will be helpful for us to explore the role of the porous nature of HC on the configuration and eventual effect of the Helmholtz plane.</p>
            
            <p>The EDLC of HC-2 and HC-3 anode materials were further expedited by symmetrical cell with the same test condition as that of HC 1 (Figure S12). The linear fitted results of HC-2 and HC-3 (Figure S13) demonstrate that as the total pore volume increases, an evident increase in EDLC can be observed, which is mainly attributed to the capacitive adsorption of pore structures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0034_R_d280575594e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Environ. Sci.">34</a>, <a href="#anie202407717-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0035_R_d280575594e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">35</a></sup></span> As expected, regardless of the pore structures of HC anodes, the EDLC in PTD electrolyte is always far smaller than that in PD electrolyte, consistently demonstrating the increased distance of the Stern layer due to the adsorption of TFEP on the HC electrode and its interaction with PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions in the inner Helmholtz layer.</p>
            
            <p>Both experimental and simulation tests have convincingly demonstrated that TFEP can effectively reorganize the IHP of HC anodes under practical conditions, thereby constructing an anion-rich electrode/electrolyte interface. For a better visual understanding, the simplified working mechanism is schematically illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0002">2H</a>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202407717-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202407717-sec-0005-title">2.3 Induced F-Rich Electrode/Electrolyte Interface</h3>
            
            <p>In view that the properties of SEI film are highly dependent on the electrode/electrolyte interface, more characterizations were carried out to investigate the differences between SEI films formed in anion-rich (PTD) and solvent-rich (PD) interfaces. Electrochemical impendence spectrum (EIS) was utilized as a sensitive tool to comprehensively assess the quality of electrode/electrolyte interface properties. Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3A</a> displays the EIS results of HC anodes after 20 cycles in PD and PTD electrolytes. The impedance observed in the PD-based cell is evidently larger than that in the PTD-based cell (more than 10 times), implying that the SEI film formed in PTD electrolyte is capable of effectively constructing a stable interface and suppressing the side reactions during cycling.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202407717-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/20dfb7bb-74ff-4b61-8385-1500be6b6a7f/anie202407717-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/20dfb7bb-74ff-4b61-8385-1500be6b6a7f/anie202407717-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/163ae5fa-c4c1-4f56-87eb-1b9f4b45eb0d/anie202407717-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/20dfb7bb-74ff-4b61-8385-1500be6b6a7f/anie202407717-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202407717-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202407717" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(A) The EIS curves of Na||HC cells with PD and PTD electrolytes after 20 cycles. (B–C) The depth profiles for NaF<sup>−</sup> and the 3D distribution of NaF<sup>−</sup> resolved by TOF-SIMS in PD and PTD electrolytes; (D–E) XPS spectra (C 1s and F 1s) of HC anodes cycling in PD and PTD electrolyte after 10 cycles; (F–G) SEM pictures of HC anodes cycling in PD and PTD electrolyte after 10 cycles (inset is the TEM pictures of SEI film); (H–I) The Young's modulus distribution of SEI formed in PD and PTD electrolyte (inset is the height image by AFM).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To investigate the root cause for the tremendous discrepancy of the interface in PD and PTD electrolytes, Time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) was first employed to analyze the SEI structure formed in PD and PTD electrolytes. The characteristic ionic fragments of NaF were chosen as the primary focus. Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3B</a> displays the depth profile of NaF<sup>−</sup> fragment, where the NaF<sup>−</sup> content in PTD electrolyte system is several times higher than that in PD system. Even after a sputtering time of 200 s, the abundance of NaF<sup>−</sup> remains consistently double. The corresponding three-dimensional (3D) structure and spatial distribution of NaF<sup>−</sup> species are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3C</a>. For PD electrolyte, NaF<sup>−</sup> is sparsely and unevenly distributed, particularly in the top layer of SEI, showing significantly less presence along the thickness. Conversely, for PTD electrolyte system, there's a persistent distribution of NaF<sup>−</sup> observed as the sputtering depth increases, hinting that remarkable anion are dragged in the inner layer by TFEP and decomposed as fundamental components of SEI.</p>
            
            <p>The chemical composition of SEI film was further resolved via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) characterizations. As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3D</a>, the C 1s spectrum of PD showcases a peak at 283.3 eV, assigned as Na<sub>x</sub>C,<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0036_R_d280575594e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Commun.">36</a>-<a href="#anie202407717-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0038_R_d280575594e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ChemElectroChem">38</a></sup></span> demonstrating the direct embedding of some Na<sup>+</sup> ions within the hard carbon interlamination before the formation of SEI layer. These “dead Na<sup>+</sup>” are prone to induce dendrite growth, puncturing the SEI, leading to continuous SEI generation and significant gas evolution. Instead, the characteristic peak of Na<sub>x</sub>C is not observed in PTD (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3E</a>). Moreover, the enhanced peak density of −CF<sub>2</sub>−CH<sub>2</sub>− (decomposition products of TFEP, as it low LUMO energy level), NaF and PO<sub>x</sub>F<sub>y</sub> (decomposition species of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions) in PTD is much higher than those in PD, implying that the decomposition of TFEP and anions effectively promotes the construction of a F-rich interface, which is consistent with the results of TOF-SIMS.</p>
            
            <p>The superiority of anion-derived SEI film was further validated through scanning electron microscope (SEM), high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), and atomic force microscope (AFM). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3F</a>, contrary to the rough and cracked morphology with dendrites observed from the surface of HC in PD electrolyte after cycling, the surface of HC in PTD exhibits a fairly smooth appearance (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3G</a>). The HRTEM inset in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3F</a> delineates a thick and heterogeneous SEI film generated on the surface of HC anode in PD, contrasting with the homogeneous SEI film with a thickness of 9 nm in PTD (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3G</a> inset).</p>
            
            <p>AFM analysis was also performed to inspect the uniformity of SEI layers. Notably, the surface of HC cycled in PTD exhibits a much better homogeneity, as indicated in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3H</a>–<a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">I</a>. The height distribution of the HC surface in PD electrolyte concentrates on a sizable range of 0–149.1 nm in a 0.8×0.8 μm<sup>2</sup> area (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3H</a> inset). However, a significantly lessened range of 0–88.3 nm in PTD is observed (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3I</a> inset). Young's modulus was measured to evaluate the mechanical properties of SEI films formed in the two different electrolytes, the modulus distribution histograms of SEI film are provided in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">3H</a>–<a href="#anie202407717-fig-0003">I</a>. Clearly, the average and maximum of Young's modulus of SEI formed in the PTD is 2.3 GPa and 6.6 GPa, nearly 4 times the value of that in PD. This substantial enhancement suggests a remarkably improved mechanical strength of anion-derived SEI, benefiting from the abundant decomposition products of TFEP and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions. The above experimental results and theoretical simulations consistently confirmed the reconstruction of Helmholtz plane by TFEP induces an anion-rich electrode/electrolyte interface and then hastens an F-rich SEI film during cycling.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202407717-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202407717-sec-0006-title">2.4 Reduced SEI Dissolution within F-Rich Interface</h3>
            
            <p>Given the significant variations in the solubility of SEI films with different compositions in the electrolyte, which would consequently influence the electrochemical performance, we quantified the dissolution degree of SEI layers in the two different electrolytes by the method proposed in Reza Younesi's work.<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0039_R_d280575594e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">39</a></sup></span> Specifically, we established a two-electrode system (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0004">4A</a>, Cu foil as cathode and Na metal as anode), and the operating voltage range of Na||Cu cell is stipulated as 0.005–2.0 V to generate intact SEI layers on the Cu foil. Subsequently, the cell charged to 2.0 V is set for a certain time (5–50 h) after every 5 cycles to unveil the situation of SEI dissolution. After every repose time, distinct capacity rises are observed, as a certain dissolution of SEI would undertake during the suspending time, the electrolyte spontaneously decomposes and offsets the loss of SEI film. Thus, the capacity differences before and after each suspension help measure the SEI dissolution in the electrolyte, to mitigate the occurrence of random errors, parallel experiments were conducted. As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0004">4B</a>–<a href="#anie202407717-fig-0004">C</a>, after a long suspension (50 h), the capacity loss in PD electrolyte (15.8/15.7 μAh) is nearly 2.5 times larger than that in PTD electrolyte (6.8/4.6 μAh), hinting that the F-rich SEI formed in PTD reduces the dissolution in organic electrolyte. As expected, in the succeeding suspension tests, the capacity loss in PD electrolyte is several times that of in PTD electrolyte all along.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202407717-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/08ee2356-11f7-4214-9b74-001d77c7e0ba/anie202407717-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/08ee2356-11f7-4214-9b74-001d77c7e0ba/anie202407717-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6d2e710d-93fb-48d1-bc25-e9cc39bc8c01/anie202407717-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/08ee2356-11f7-4214-9b74-001d77c7e0ba/anie202407717-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202407717-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202407717" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(A) The models of Na||Cu cells for SEI dissolution tests; (B–C) The capacity loss for different pause times in Na||Cu cells with PD and PTD electrolytes; (D–E) The resistance of SEI films in Na||HC half cells with PD and PTD electrolytes after different setting pause; (F) The differences of the atomic ratios of SEI composition in PD and PTD electrolytes between cycled HC and cycled-soaked HC anodes.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Considering the severe dissolution observed on smooth Cu foil, it is speculated that the real situations on rough and porous hard carbon surface would be more serious. Hence, Na||HC half-cell was assembled for further investigation. All the cells were conducted 5 pre-cycles to form SEI and were subjected to EIS testing at set intervals. As well recognized, the semicircle at high frequency corresponds to the resistance of the SEI layer (R<sub>SEI</sub>),<span><sup><a href="#anie202407717-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0040_R_d280575594e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">40</a>-<a href="#anie202407717-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202407717-bib-0042_R_d280575594e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span> thus the value change of R<sub>SEI</sub> pithily informs the state of SEI layers. As depicted in Figure S14 and Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0004">4D</a>–<a href="#anie202407717-fig-0004">E</a>, the value of R<sub>SEI</sub> in PD electrolyte delivered an exaggerated increment with the increasing setting time, elucidating that the SEI suffers a grievous damage. In contrast, the value of R<sub>SEI</sub> in PTD almost remains unchanged, indicating that the NaF-rich SEI is still stable in the electrolyte even under the harsher real operating conditions.</p>
            
            <p>An intensive inquiry of the chemical compositions of SEI before and after dissolving was assisted by XPS (detailed information is provided in the experimental section). The HC anodes were precycled for five cycles in Na||HC half cells to form SEI films. Compared to the enormous atomic ratio changes of SEI composition soaked in PC/DEC solvents (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0004">4F</a>), only a puny atomic adjustment is observed in PC/TFEP/DEC solvents. Figure S15 exhibits the C 1s spectrum of SEI component after soaking. Signally, in PC/DEC solvents, the intensity of the Na<sub>x</sub>C peak located at 283.3 eV is greatly raised (even stronger than the C−C peak), manifesting that the SEI has been severely destroyed in the soaking period. Instead, the faint Na<sub>x</sub>C peak in PC/TFEP/DEC solvents and the high content of anion-decomposed products (PO<sub>x</sub>F<sub>y</sub> and NaF, Figure S16) provide convincing evidence that the F-rich SEI film could restrain the dissolution and greatly optimize the electrode/electrolyte interface.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202407717-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202407717-sec-0007-title">2.5 Enhanced Electrochemical Performance of HC Anode</h3>
            
            <p>To further explore the specific impacts of different interfacial properties on the electrochemical performance of HC anodes, Na||HC half-cells with PD and PTD electrolytes were assembled (all the HC anodes for electrochemical tests are HC 1). Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">5A</a>–<a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">B</a> exhibits the cycling performance of HC anode in both PD and PTD electrolytes. The half-cell with PD electrolyte encounters a severe capacity attenuation (&lt;62.0 % after 100 cycles) accompanied by a lower Coulombic efficiency (~99.6 %). By contrast, the half-cell with PTD electrolyte exhibits superb cycling stability, offering a 94.1 % capacity retention after 400 cycles and maintaining an average Coulombic efficiency of over 99.9 % under a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of HC electrodes at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">5C</a>–<a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">D</a> demonstrate that the reversible capacities in the first cycle are 271.8 and 271.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the initial Coulombic efficiencies (ICEs) are 82.4 % and 82.7 % for PTD and PD -based half cells, respectively. Once the current density rises to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the cell with PD electrolyte undergoes a huge capacity loss after merely 50 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">5C</a>), while the cell using PTD still maintains great electrochemical stability (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">5D</a>). The enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics is confirmed by the GITT test in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">5E</a>–<a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">F</a>. The diffusion coefficient of HC could be roughly divided into adsorption process and intercalation process. In PTD the coefficients are basically located within the range of 10<sup>−5</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> to 10<sup>−7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (adsorption) and 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> to 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (intercalation), which are almost 2 orders of magnitude higher than that in PD electrolyte.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202407717-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/27c80f9a-00cc-4354-beb8-3409ffabbe59/anie202407717-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/27c80f9a-00cc-4354-beb8-3409ffabbe59/anie202407717-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2f90942d-bd6a-43b1-a486-31f0c3ccc436/anie202407717-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/27c80f9a-00cc-4354-beb8-3409ffabbe59/anie202407717-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202407717-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202407717" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(A–B) Cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency of Na||HC half cells with PTD and PD electrolytes. The specific current density is 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the first three cycles and 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the subsequent cycles. The voltage range is 0.01–2.0 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>); (C–D) The charge and discharge curves of HC anodes in PD and PTD electrolytes at different cycles; (E–F) GITT curves and diffusion coefficients of Na||HC half cells with PTD and PD electrolytes; (G–H) CV curves of Na||HC half cells using PD and PTD electrolytes with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Furthermore, Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">5G</a>–<a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">H</a> compares the CV curves of both half cells measured at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage range of 0.01–2.0 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Despite showing similar oxidation peak intensity in the first cycle, the cell with PD electrolyte displays significantly weakened peaks in the subsequent cycles, and the peaks nearly disappear at the third cycle (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">5G</a>). Sharply contrast, the oxidation peak intensity in PTD exhibited notably enhanced oxidation peak intensity, demonstrating inspiring stability over the following two scans (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0005">5H</a>). The comparison convincingly illustrates that TFEP markedly improves the electrochemical compatibility of electrolytes with HC anode, enabling the half-cell to achieve a desirable cycling performance.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202407717-sec-0008">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202407717-sec-0008-title">2.6 2.8 Ah HC||NFPP Pouch Cell with PTD Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>Prior to the construction of a full battery system, it's imperative to assess the compatibility between PTD electrolyte and cathode materials. We first measured the electrochemical stable window of electrolytes by linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) with Na||Al cells. As shown in Figure S17, the upper voltage limit of PTD electrolyte reaches 5.40 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), which is far higher than that of PD electrolyte (4.41 V), indicating that the introduction of TFEP significantly widens the electrochemical stable window. Subsequently, the electrochemical performance of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP) cathode was evaluated in a Na||NFPP half-cell with PTD electrolyte. As shown in Figure S18, NFPP exhibits an inspiring cycling stability (96 % capacity retention after 2000 cycles) with high Coulombic efficiency (~99.9 %) at a current density of 5 C (1 C=129 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). Moreover, the half-cell also manifests impressive rate performance, a specific capacity of 72 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is still attained even under an ultra-high current density of 20 C (Figure S19).</p>
            
            <p>Moreover, the electrochemical compatibility of PTD with high-voltage Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) cathode material was also evaluated in Na||NVPF half cell. As can be seen in Figure S20–21, Na||NVPF half cell with PTD electrolyte exhibits excellent cycling stability (93.2 % capacity retention after 500 cycles) with a stable operating voltage of ~3.55 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). By contrast, the half cell using PD electrolyte suffers severe capacity decay and voltage attenuation in 100 cycles. The comparison demonstrates that PTD can work as a high-voltage electrolyte for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            
            <p>Then, 2.8 Ah pouch cell based on HC anode and NFPP cathode was constructed to further validate the industrial application potential of PTD electrolyte. Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0006">6A</a> illustrates the cycling performance of the cells with PD and PTD electrolytes. Inspiringly, the HC||NFPP pouch cell with PTD attained a capacity retention of 90 % after 600 cycles at 0.5 C (1 C=3 A g<sup>−1</sup>). Note that the average discharge voltage shows little decline compared to the initial state (2.87 V <i>vs</i>. 2.84 V, Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0006">6B</a>) even after 600 cycles, implying impressive applicability of PTD electrolyte in the pouch cell. Conversely, the cell with PD electrolyte exhibits much inferior cycling performance, suffering a sudden capacity/CE drop after only 200 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0006">6C</a> and Figure S22). The poor cycle life could be attributed to the serious volume change caused by the side reactions and severe gas production during cycling (Figure S23A). Instead, the pouch cell with PTD doesn't bloat even after 600 cycles (Figure S23B). Except for the splendid cycle life, the robust and thin electrode/electrolyte interface also provides an unobstructed channel for rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0006">6D</a>, a much more improved rate performance in PTD is observed, with the cell all exhibiting a higher capacity and retention rate at the same current. Even at the current up to 3 C (9 A g<sup>−1</sup>), the capacity retention of the cell with PTD still reaches 83 %. Besides, the working potential is also much higher than that with PD electrolyte at the same current rates (Figure S24). The pouch cell with PTD offers an operating voltage of over 2.3 V at 9 A, while it drops below 2.0 V for the cell with PD electrolyte.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202407717-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/715729a0-da73-4e94-86de-87c4e17d820a/anie202407717-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/715729a0-da73-4e94-86de-87c4e17d820a/anie202407717-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a04b0cca-5fb6-447a-830e-556e4bf54769/anie202407717-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/715729a0-da73-4e94-86de-87c4e17d820a/anie202407717-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202407717-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202407717" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(A) Cycling performance of 2.8 Ah HC||NFPP pouch cells with PTD and PD electrolytes. The current is 1500 mA and the voltage range is 2.0–3.8 V; (B–C) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of pouch cells using PD and PTD electrolytes with different cycles at 0.5 C (1 C=3000 mA); (D) Rate performance of pouch cells with different electrolytes. The cells are charged at 0.2 C and discharged at corresponding current; (E) Optical photograph and temperature changes of pouch cell (fully charged) before, and after the nail penetration tests.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The weakened interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and TFEP (compared to Na<sup>+</sup>-PC) endows PTD electrolyte with a higher transference number (0.52) of Na<sup>+</sup> in contrast to that in PD (0.34, Figure S25). Figure S26 presents the ionic conductivities of NaPF<sub>6</sub>-EC/DEC, PD, and PTD electrolytes in the temperature range from −60 to 60 °C. After the addition of TFEP, the ionic conductivity of electrolytes decreases slightly. Nevertheless, compared with commercial NaPF<sub>6</sub>-EC/DEC electrolyte, PTD exhibits a far superior low-temperature adaptability (&gt;0.5 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at −40 °C). The enhanced kinetics and wide liquid temperature range lay a solid foundation for its low-temperature applications. Figure S27 shows the low-temperature performance of pouch cells with both electrolytes. The cell with PTD delivers capacities of 1.82 Ah (65 % capacity retention) and 1.65 Ah (58.9 % capacity retention) with operating voltages of 1.96 V and 1.64 V at −40 and −50 °C, respectively. For comparison, the cell with PD electrolyte only offers a capacity of 1.22 Ah (43.5 % capacity retention) and an average working voltage of 1.44 V at −40 °C.</p>
            
            <p>Then, the HC||NFPP pouch cells were charged and discharged at −20 °C with a current density of 0.1 C (250 mA) in PD and PTD electrolytes to simulate the practical low- temperature conditions, both cells were precycled at 30 °C for three cycles before low-temperature cycling tests. As depicted in Figure S28A, the cell with PD suffers severe capacity attenuation (&lt;20 % capacity retention after 3 cycles) owing to the Na plating anodic reaction at low temperature. By contrast, the cell with PTD electrolyte shows a greatly improved low-temperature adaptability (Figure S28B) benefiting from the weak interaction between Li<sup>+</sup> and TFEP. The realization of continuous splendid operation performance at subzero temperature demonstrates that PTD can serve as a potential low-temperature electrolyte for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            
            <p>Regarding safety considerations, the flammability of PTD and PD electrolytes was comparatively evaluated by ignition tests. As shown in Figure S29, the PTD-soaked membrane does not support combustion at all, which is significantly different from PD electrolyte-soaked membrane. Notably, nail penetration, known as the harshest battery safety test, was conducted on a fully-charged 2.8 Ah HC||NFPP pouch cell with PTD. Figure <a href="#anie202407717-fig-0006">6E</a> shows the optical photograph and temperature changes of cells before and after the test. Obviously, the cell with PD electrolyte exhibits a bit bloat and a serious temperature increase (from 25.0 to 83.0 °C), reversely, the cell using PTD electrolyte remains intact without any deformation and the temperature rise is also effectively restrained (51.2 °C), hinting that the pouch cell with PTD electrolyte has a high safety. To the best of our knowledge, low-concentration electrolyte with such high electrochemical compatibility, superior low-temperature adaptability and enhanced safety has never been reported before in SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202407717-sec-0009">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202407717-sec-0009-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, to realize the reconstruction of Helmholtz plane, we assess the potential solvents by two key factors (PFBE and CAE) and determine TFEP as the optimized solvent. Then the working mechanism of TFEP molecule is disclosed, TFEP preferentially absorbs on the HC anode surface and then drag the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions into the inner Helmholtz layer by the strong intermolecular interaction between TFEP and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, finally realizing the construction of anion-rich IHP on HC anode. The induced anion-derived F-rich SEI film not only strengthens the mechanical stability of the interface upon repeated cycling, but also enables fast Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics. The PTD electrolyte shows a great electrochemical compatibility with HC anode (&gt;90 % capacity retention after 400 cycles). More significantly, it endows the 2.8 Ah HC||NFPP pouch cell with excellent cycling stability (90 % capacity retention after 600 cycles), outstanding rate capability (83 % capacity retention at 3 C) and terrific low-temperature adaptability (65 % capacity retention at −40 °C). Note that the nonflammable TFEP also enhances the battery safety (pass the nail penetration test). We believe this novel understanding of regulating interfacial properties will provide guidance for the design of compatible LCEs and promote the development of low-cost, long-life and high-safe sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
